  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8c8db45f-a05d-4539-9c77-10ee0e65c4cf'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer11535118568924626870">"...what man wins more happiness than just its shape and the ruin when that shape collapses?"<br/><br/>Sophocles' Oedipus Rex has never been surpassed for the raw and terrible power with which its hero struggles to answer the eternal question, "Who am I?" The play, a story of a king who acting entirely in ignorance kills his father and marries his mother, unfolds with shattering pow</span>
  <span id="freeText11535118568924626870" style="display:none">"...what man wins more happiness than just its shape and the ruin when that shape collapses?"<br /><br />Sophocles' Oedipus Rex has never been surpassed for the raw and terrible power with which its hero struggles to answer the eternal question, "Who am I?" The play, a story of a king who acting entirely in ignorance kills his father and marries his mother, unfolds with shattering power; we are helplessly carried along with Oedipus towards the final, horrific truth. <br /><br />To make Oedipus more accessible for the modern reader, our Prestwick House Literary Touchstone Classics includes a glossary of the more difficult words, as well as convenient sidebar notes to enlighten the reader on aspects that may be confusing or overlooked. We hope that the reader may, through this edition, more fully enjoy the beauty of the verse, the wisdom of the insights, and the impact of the drama.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11535118568924626870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1463207-i-think-that-a-quote-isn-t-right-as-i">I think that a quote isn&#39;t right, as I can&#39;t find it in the text. “I have no desire to suffer twice, in reality and then in retrospect.”

Where is that from? Where can I find it in Oedipus Rex, if it is there?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1463207'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F1554.Oedipus_Rex">like</a></span>

</li>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1463207-i-think-that-a-quote-isn-t-right-as-i">Add your answer</a>
</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/4601204-daniel"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_100x100-c7bfe730633c9983e27caaa66a8e244a.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/4601204-daniel">Daniel</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The quote may be from a different translation. Translations can differ to a surprising degree. I would look for a similar line in the translation that<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The quote may be from a different translation. Translations can differ to a surprising degree. I would look for a similar line in the translation that you have and go from there. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=ancient">ancient</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=greece">greece</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=classics-literay-fiction">classics-literay-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=read-years-ago">read-years-ago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30575403-lizzy?shelf=stars-5">stars-5</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1715582844" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1870121809723861031">What can I say about <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles' title='Sophocles' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sophocles</a>’ <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1554.Oedipus_Rex__The_Theban_Plays___1_' title='Oedipus Rex (The Theban Plays, #1) by Sophocles' rel='nofollow noopener'>Oedipus Rex</a> that has not already been said? Apart from the patricide and the infamous incest, this is an ancient tale of angst and overall calamity. But since I recently revisited it, this legendary <i>tragedy</i> hasn’t left my mind. <br/><blockquote>
  <i>"Look and learn all citizens of Thebes. This is Oedipus.<br/>He, who read the famous riddle, and we hailed chief of men,<br/>All envied his power, glory, and good fortune.<br/>Now upon his head the sea of disaster crashes down.”</i>
</blockquote><br/>I felt after reading th</span>
  <span id="freeText1870121809723861031" style="display:none">What can I say about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a>’ <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1554.Oedipus_Rex__The_Theban_Plays___1_" title="Oedipus Rex (The Theban Plays, #1) by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Oedipus Rex</a> that has not already been said? Apart from the patricide and the infamous incest, this is an ancient tale of angst and overall calamity. But since I recently revisited it, this legendary <i>tragedy</i> hasn’t left my mind. <br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Look and learn all citizens of Thebes. This is Oedipus.<br />He, who read the famous riddle, and we hailed chief of men,<br />All envied his power, glory, and good fortune.<br />Now upon his head the sea of disaster crashes down.”</i>
</blockquote><br />I felt after reading the play that there was not really anything that Oedipus could have done to get himself out of his destiny. In fact, it seems that the more he attempts to get out of it, the deeper he is immersed in its inevitability. It is simply that there was no way for him to avoid doing it all and facing his fate. After hearing of the prophecy he flees because he doesn't want it to come true, but there is a lot that he does not know and a lot that he is not being told. His parents, when told by the oracles decided to sacrifice him. But he was saved by the compassionate nature of humanity. Later on, his step parents also leave him in ignorance, and in hiding the truth they are also making the prophecy come true. <br /><br />The theme as I see it, therefore, is of fate versus freewill. However, there really does not seem to be any freewill here. Every decision that Oedipus makes only brings the revelation closer to being fulfilled.<br /><br />But to fully understand Sophocles work, you have to know that for the ancient Greeks the word "tragedy" didn't mean <i>“a lamentable, dreadful, or fatal event or affair; calamity; disaster.”</i> For them the idea of such a play, that had a certain and defined theme and structure, is about a person that because of a single tragic flaw becomes the victim of the gods. The specific purpose was called <i>"catharsis"</i>, the audience watching the play should gain an emotional release that made your own <i>trivial</i> issues fade into insignificance. According to <i>Aristotle’s Poetics “the complexity of the plot is established through reversal, recognition and suffering.”</i> The tragedy is created, in part, by the complexity of its plot which leads towards the catharsis. The Chorus is crucial; its speeches are revealing. It is the cautious voice of collective wisdom. And from the very beginning of the play, the Chorus revealed the omen of disaster. This can all be summed up in the following lines:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"O god-<br />All come true, all busting to light!<br />O light- now let me look my last on you!<br />I stand revealed at last-”</i>
</blockquote><br />Oedipus is a passionate man, who asks questions and takes risks. Despite his flaws and his sins, Oedipus is good and always seeks the truth no matter how devastating. In the end, he accepts the responsibility for his actions, his fate and punishment. Does he have free will or the ability to choose his own path or is everything in his life been predetermined? Indeed, despite the prophecy, it can never be denied that Oedipus and his parents had made the choices, not the oracle or the Gods. Is the very idea of carving out your eyes, after discovering your wife is your mother in this incredibly packed tragedy that alleviates so much the enormous pain that seems so causeless? Is the existential angst finally satisfies by the human need to identify the guilty that alleviates our human sensation of utter, senseless and chaotic misery? <br /><br />This is what torments us, being humans: we have free will but we can never control everything. Oedipus’s specific life events aren’t exactly relatable to any of us, but the sensations are not less pertinent. Aren’t we used to impending unconquerable doom? I ask myself, could ignorance lead us through hell? <i>Oedipus Rex</i> doesn’t make us only question the role of the gods (or whatever may decide our fate nowadays: politicians, the economy, the news, and even our own expectations!), but above all the argument of fate and destiny, and whether we are able to live without external powers deciding our chances. It also makes us question who we are; whether our personalities, or other personal characteristics, are a kind of destiny in itself. <br /><br />Where's our human freedom? More important: do you feel a prevailing sense of inevitability, no matter what you do?! Why are we always being judged, by ourselves and by the world? If we try to transpose the play to today, many questions are still left with no definite answers. For certain, we can choose what we want to become. The curse is that our capacities are finite; we are not gods. What happened to Oedipus was the torture of being human, can we escape this curse? <br /><br /><i>Oedipus Rex</i> is a literary masterpiece! Highly recommended!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1870121809723861031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10415148628193707467">Οἰδίπους Τύραννος = Oedipus Tyrannus = Oedipus = Oedipus Rex = Oedipus the King (The Theban Plays #1), Sophocles <br/>Oedipus the King, is an Athenian tragedy by Sophoclesو that was first performed around 429 BC.   <span id="freeText10415148628193707467" style="display:none">Οἰδίπους Τύραννος = Oedipus Tyrannus = Oedipus = Oedipus Rex = Oedipus the King (The Theban Plays #1), Sophocles <br />Oedipus the King, is an Athenian tragedy by Sophoclesو that was first performed around 429 BC. Oedipus sent his brother-in-law Creon to ask advice of the oracle at Delphi concerning a plague ravaging Thebes. Creon returns to report that the plague is the result of religious pollution, since the murderer of their former King, Laius, had never been caught. Oedipus vows to find the murderer and curses him for causing the plague. ...<br /><br />عنوانها: ادیپ شهریار؛ ادیبوس شاه؛ ادیبوس شهریار؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بیست و ششم ماه آگوست سال 1974 میلادی<br />عنوان: ادیپ شهریار؛ نویسنده: سوفوکلس؛ مترجم: شاهرخ مسکوب؛ در 96 ص؛ چاپ دوم با عنوان: ادیب در کلنوس؛ در 212 ص؛ موضوع: نمایشنامه های نویسندگان یونانی - قرن پنجم پیش از میلاد<br />عنوان: ادیپ شهریار؛ نویسنده: سوفوکل؛ مترجم: فاطمه عربی؛ استهبان، 1387؛ در 72 ص؛ شابک: 9786005209006؛<br />عنوان: ادیبوس شاه؛ مترجم: ساسان قاسمی؛ تهران، جعفری، 1387؛ در 132 ص؛ شابک: 9789646088733؛ <br />عنوان: ادیبوس شهریار؛ مترجم: تهران، پژواک کیوان؛ 1389؛ در 144 ص؛ شابک: 9789648727890؛<br /><br />قسمتی از نمایشنامه<br />.همسرایان: می‌خواهیم حقیقت هیاهویی را که تا به امروز بر سر زبانهاست بدانیم<br />..ادیپوس: وای بر من<br />.همسرایان: آرام باش تمنا می‌کنم<br />..ادیپوس: بسیار ناهنجار است. باری می‌گویم. من نارواترین بیداد را بر خود هموار کردم. من ستمی ناسزاوار بر خود هموار کردم. خدا می‌داند که اختیاری در کار نبود<br />.همسرایان: در چه کاری؟<br />..ادیپوس: در ازدواجی ننگین به خاطر شرم، نادانسته به زناشویی رسوایی دست زدم<br />.همسرایان: می‌گویند مادرت در این پیوند ننگین همسر تو بود<br />..ادیپوس: بیاد آوردن آن در حکم مرگ من است. تازه این دو نیز(آنتیگنه و ایسمنه) از آن منند<br />.همسرایان: نه<br />..ادیپوس: فرزندان نفرین شده<br />.همسرایان: آه، خدایا<br />..ادیپوس: و میوه‌های بطن همان مادر<br />.همسرایان: دختران تو و<br />..ادیپوس: خواهرانم! آه خواهران پدر خود<br />.همسرایان: آیا پدرت را<br />..ادیپوس: باز هم رنجی دیگر و شکنجه‌ای تازه؟<br />.همسرایان: تو او را کشتی؟<br />..ادیپوس: آری اما به حق<br />.همسرایان: به حق؟<br />..ادیپوس: آری (ناشناخته، در راه) کسی را کشتم که می‌خواست مرا بکشد<br /><br />داستان نمایشنامه<br />تقدیر چنین مقرر کرده‌ که «ادیپ» شهریار، پدر خود را بکشد، و با مادر خویش همبستر شود. فرمانی ست ظالمانه و دوزخی. این حکم را پدر و مادر ادیب دریافته‌ اند، و برای گریختن از آن، «ادیپ» کودک را به چوپانی می‌سپارند، تا جانش را بگیرد. اما اگر ریختن خون طفلی، بر پدر و مادر او دشوار آید، بر چوپان ساده دلی نیز آسان نیست؛ چوپان لبخند معصومانه ی کودک را می‌بیند، و او را به چوپانی دیگر، از دیار «کرنت» می‌سپارد. شبان دوم او را نزد شاه کشور خویش می‌برد، و کودک در دربار آن شاه بزرگ می‌شود. «ادیب» در دوران جوانی به وسیله ی هاتفان، از سرنوشت خود آگاه می‌شود، و چون پدرخوانده، و مادرخوانده‌ اش را پدر و مادر حقیقی خود می‌پندارد، برای گریز از سرنوشت، از آن دیار می‌گریزد. در راه به گردونه ی پیرمردی می‌رسد. پس از گفتگویی کوتاه، پیرمرد را (که پدر واقعی او بوده) می‌کشد، و به سوی شهر «تب» می‌تازد. بر دروازه ی آن شهر از دیرگاه ابوالهولی بوده، که از مردمان معمائی می‌پرسیده، و چون آنان در پاسخ درمی‌ماندند، طعمه ی مرگ می‌شدند. «ادیب» معمای ابوالهول (نماینده تقدیر) را پاسخ درست می‌گوید، و ابوالهول بر خاک می‌افتد. ساکنان شهر «تب» به پاس این گره‌گشائی، شهریاری دیار خود را به «ادیپ» می‌بخشند، و دست ملکه ی شهر (مادرادیپ) را، در دست او می‌گذارند. پس از سالها فرمانروائی، مرگ، و طاعون بر آن شهر فرود می‌آید، و چون «ادیب» خود سبب آن فاجعه را از معبد کاهنان آپولو می‌پرسد، پاسخ می‌شنود که گناهکار باید از میانه برخیزد، گناهکاری که پدر خود را کشته و با مادرش هم‌بستر شده‌ است. ادیپ در جستجوی گناهکار پلید، پس از ماجراهایی، سرانجام به خود می‌رسد، و چشمهای جهان بین خویش را برمی‌کند. ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_924626115" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/924626115" />
    <a title="Renato Magalhães Rocha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha"><img alt="Renato Magalhães Rocha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524097330p2/29402621.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/924626115?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Renato Magalhães Rocha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Renato Magalhães Rocha" href="/user/show/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha">Renato Magalhães Rocha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 8525409146" href="/book/show/4170733-dipo-rei">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=100-best-books-world-library">100-best-books-world-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=quickies">quickies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=all-stars">all-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=the-drama-100">the-drama-100</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer924626115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer406040582852931992"><blockquote>
  <i>"Look and learn all citizens of Thebes. This is Oedipus.<br/>He, who read the famous riddle, and we hailed chief of men,<br/>All envied his power, glory, and good fortune.<br/>Now upon his head the sea of disaster crashes down.<br/>Mortality is man’s burden. Keep your eyes fixed on your last day.<br/>Call no man happy until he reaches it, and finds rest from suffering."</i>
</blockquote><br/>I believe that in one way or another, everyone - at least to some extent - has heard of the story of Oedipus and Jocasta. It's one of those tales tha</span>
  <span id="freeText406040582852931992" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"Look and learn all citizens of Thebes. This is Oedipus.<br />He, who read the famous riddle, and we hailed chief of men,<br />All envied his power, glory, and good fortune.<br />Now upon his head the sea of disaster crashes down.<br />Mortality is man’s burden. Keep your eyes fixed on your last day.<br />Call no man happy until he reaches it, and finds rest from suffering."</i>
</blockquote><br />I believe that in one way or another, everyone - at least to some extent - has heard of the story of Oedipus and Jocasta. It's one of those tales that's been on our collective consciousness forever even though we may not even be able to assertively answer about its origins. The same might be said, for example, of Odysseus and Don Quixote: they've been so used and re-used, adapted and re-adapted throughout so many generations and in so many different formats that one might as well state they were simply born within us, for they're public and common knowledge. I, for one, believed Oedipus and Jocasta's tale came from the Bible! As I was never a religious person and therefore never payed much attention to it - and unfortunately never decently studied Greek mythology -, I used to unconsciously attribute to the Bible the origins of all stories which seemed to me as too ancient to properly date. I'm terribly sorry and embarrassed about that, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a>. I stand corrected now.<br /><br />Every time I read an ancient text I recurrently find myself to blame because of the same mistake: being surprised by its quality despite being written so long ago. It turns out more and more I agree with an analysis I've read somewhere that states that, unlike science, there is no progress, no discovery in art. An artist, while he creates, is not helped by the efforts of all the others - like scientists are - and depends upon his own individual truths. The ancient art is in no way a primitive version of the art created by our contemporaries. So it should not be astonishing to me that a text written thousands of years ago possesses the same qualities or refinement of awarded pieces that only now cracked their fifty years of age mark.<br /><br />Putting the story itself a little aside, it's precisely this refinement, this brilliance in the construction of the narrative that impressed me so much. The pace, the development of the action and disentanglement of this intricate plot was written so masterfully that it requires little investigation in discovering the reasons why it became so influential to the subsequent generations. Now, I'm not knowledgeable enough to affirm that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a> himself wasn't influenced by other works that preceded him, so I'm not claiming unprecedented originality to his name here, but merely(!) talent in using the most appropriate techniques to write so many wondrous predicates into this marvelous play. The ability with which he created, sustained and solved the various mysteries that surround this classical tragedy is very remarkable, as well as a striking mixture of pity and horror that the themes developed here successfully imposes on the reader.<br /><br />Themes such as fate, free will, interference in human life by the Gods (for some that hasn't changed much, has it?) and its inflexible exploration of human nature and suffering are skillfully written in the form of intense dialogues and shocking revelations that could even prove too disturbing had not been <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a> accurate treatment, much like the reader likely pities Phedre's actions instead of automatically blaming her for her fate. The ever so mesmerizing battle between destiny and logical consequences also plays a big role here: does fate completely control Oedipus's actions - is it all predetermined? -, or is he simply a victim of his own doings, even if unknowingly?<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1554.Oedipus_Rex" title="Oedipus Rex by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Oedipus Rex</a> (also known as <i>Oedipus the King</i> and <i>Oedipus Tyrannus</i>) tells the story of Oedipus, a man that's respected and loved in Thebas, where he is King after solving the riddle of the Sphinx and marrying Jocasta, the widow of the previous king. After a plague threatens his kingdom, he is begged by a chorus of Thebans for help and Oedipus sends for an oracle in order to find some guidance. As it turns out, Tiresias, the blind prophet, believes the King is the only one to blame for his malady. At first outraged and, because of it, incensed into proving his innocence, he starts connecting the clues that he receives from various bits of information gathered by different sources. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As it turns out, Oedipus, after leaving his home in Corinth due to a prophecy which stated he would murder his father and sleep with his mother, entered a fight with some men at a crossroads and ended up killing them, before arriving in Thebes. One of these men was Laius, Jocasta’s husband and previous King. In order to escape the prophecy, Oedipus fell into it, as he was Laius’s son who was sent away to be killed many years ago exactly because he received an oracle that he would be murdered by his own son. Oedipus’s life ended up being spared and, unknown to him, he was adopted by the King of Corinth. Now it was clear to him that, besides murdering his father, he has slept with his own mother and fathered children that were also his brothers and sisters. Jocasta, upon finding out this complex imbroglio, can't deal with the unimaginable situation and kills herself. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Completely horrified and ruined by everything he found out, Oedipus blinds himself (ironically at the precise moment when he sees the whole truth) so he wouldn't ever again need to see his own feelings of shame and humiliation mirrored in the faces of the others.<br /><br />I've read some criticism stating that some of the drama in the play is a bit over the top, and while I wouldn't agree and, more importantly, couldn't possibly begin to imagine myself in the same situation, I guess it was in vogue at the time that the heroes would suffer so much when they'd find their worlds turned upside down that they would impose on themselves severe sentences such as mutilations or death. Part of their heroism is exactly accepting to endure serious consequences, not once pleading blamelessness. Even later, in Shakespeare, we were still to find six or seven characters dying just like that, entire families decimated because of the belief that there could be no way out once the universe had programmed their fates.<br /><br />Film adaptation: as influential as this story was everywhere, of course it wouldn’t lack adaptations in film. When I found out there was one <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0061613/" rel="nofollow noopener">Edipo Re</a> (1967), directed by Pasolini, I instantly picked it to watch as I imagined that controversial material filmed by controversial director could only result in very interesting movie - to say the least! Much to my surprise, the ick factor was greatly downplayed and this time the Italian director focused more on the emotional aspects of his narrative than on the sexual ones. His rendition was very faithful to the story, although the linear narrative lacked the sophistication employed by Sophocles that chose to slowly reveal details of the plot by making use of different characters referring to past events. The power of the prophecy and the influence in human lives by the Gods were also not as active as in the original story. The intro Pasolini used though was very interesting: it begins in modern days where a father is very jealous of his son's connection with his mother and decides to get rid of him, as if he was anticipating an Oedipus complex situation; after that, time goes back to the ancient days.<br /><br />Rating: I can't wait to read more from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a> and if my anticipation for the remaining plays in this trilogy (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/148313.Oedipus_at_Colonus" title="Oedipus at Colonus by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Oedipus at Colonus</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7728.Antigone" title="Antigone by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Antigone</a>) means anything, is that it's a great testament of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1554.Oedipus_Rex" title="Oedipus Rex by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Oedipus Rex</a>'s qualities and how highly I enjoyed this short but intense reading experience: 5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="406040582852931992" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1099919166" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1099919166" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1099919166?book_show_action=true">Nov 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=ancient-greece">ancient-greece</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1099919166" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15158731796128997867"><b>Sometimes life's a real bitch. </b><br/><br/>Fate is unavoidable in ancient Greek Tragedy. Trying to avoid it will only lead to it, and doing nothing will lead you there too. So if a God tells you that you will die at the hands of your son, and that he will then go on to steal your wife, you’d best do nothing because it’s going to happen anyway. Any preventative action you take will only lead to the same ending. So, you’re pretty much screwed. You might as well lie down and accept it. The God's are mean. <br/><br/>But,</span>
  <span id="freeText15158731796128997867" style="display:none"><b>Sometimes life's a real bitch. </b><br><br>Fate is unavoidable in ancient Greek Tragedy. Trying to avoid it will only lead to it, and doing nothing will lead you there too. So if a God tells you that you will die at the hands of your son, and that he will then go on to steal your wife, you’d best do nothing because it’s going to happen anyway. Any preventative action you take will only lead to the same ending. So, you’re pretty much screwed. You might as well lie down and accept it. The God's are mean. <br><br>But, nope, if you’re like the King of Thebes you’ll leave your infant son for dead instead. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1456506898i/18244383.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Poor Oedipus. He really didn’t have much chance in life. He could do nothing to intervene with his own destiny, mainly because his tragic flaw is his lack of awareness about his true origins. He hears a rumour of the prophecy told to his farther, so he endeavours to stay away from him. But, in doing so he is pushed ever closer to his real farther. That’s the problem with being abandoned at birth; you just don’t know who is who in the world! There’s some irony in this somewhere. <br><br>Indeed, it suggests that no free will exists at all because any exertions of the supposed free will lead to the predetermined fate. So every action has been accounted for already. The intended audience may have been aware of these powers but Oedipus and his farther were hapless in their wake. They had to both learn the hard way. Oedipus had to recognise it, and in the process he shattered his life: it made him tear out his very eyes. Now that’s real grief. There’s no wonder Aristotle made this his model for the perfect play because this is masterful.<br><br>Aristotle’s theory can be used to assist the reader in understanding how the plot contributes to the tragedy. I couldn’t have read tragedy without it. The tragedy is created, in part, by the complexity of its plot which leads towards the catharsis. According to Aristotle’s Poetics the complexity of the plot is established through reversal, recognition and suffering. A simple plot will only establish one of these; therefore, it will have a limited catharsis. The reversal (peritpeteia) is the change of a state of affairs to its opposite, such as the reversal of Oedipus’ identity. The recognition (anaghorsis) is achieved through the acquiring of knowledge, like the knowledge Oedipus gains of his birth. Aristotle argues that an effective plot has its anaghorisis bound up with the peritpeteia. This is because it, “carries with it pity or fear” such as these following lines:<br><br><br>		<i>"O god-<br>All come true, all busting to light!<br>O light- now let me look my last on you!<br>I stand revealed at last-”</i> (Lines 1305-9)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1456506822i/18244380._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I hope I didn’t lose anyone or bore them to death with my summary of <i>Poetics.</i> The structure is the key; it is everything in delivering the plot. If, in the cathartic moment, the action can evoke suffering through a combination of a reversal of circumstances during a brutally stark recognition, then the ultimate delivery of pity and fear will be achieved. Such is the case with Oedipus. Oedipus’s hamartia, his tragic flaw at the core of his being, is his ignorance, and when the veil is lifted he realises the tragedy of the situation; he realises all too late that fate is unshakable and unconquerable. <br><br>He has unknowingly committed incest with his mother and murdered his farther, so, like I said, life is a real bitch. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15158731796128997867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1749311121" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer1434139445847701289"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br/>4 out of 5 stars to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1554.Oedipus_Rex__The_Theban_Plays___1_' title='Oedipus Rex (The Theban Plays, #1) by Sophocles' rel='nofollow noopener'>Oedipus Rex</a>, the first of "The Theban Plays," written by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles' title='Sophocles' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sophocles</a> around 430 BC. If you are unfamiliar with Greek tragedies, the thing you need to know most is that the authors often played with the concept of fate: not just that some things are meant to be or to come back and haunt you, but that there is always more going on than you realize at the time. This is one of the plays you should absolutely read. Although borderline spoiler, it's important t</span>
  <span id="freeText1434139445847701289" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>Book Review</u>
  </i>
</b><br />4 out of 5 stars to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1554.Oedipus_Rex__The_Theban_Plays___1_" title="Oedipus Rex (The Theban Plays, #1) by Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Oedipus Rex</a>, the first of "The Theban Plays," written by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1002.Sophocles" title="Sophocles" rel="nofollow noopener">Sophocles</a> around 430 BC. If you are unfamiliar with Greek tragedies, the thing you need to know most is that the authors often played with the concept of fate: not just that some things are meant to be or to come back and haunt you, but that there is always more going on than you realize at the time. This is one of the plays you should absolutely read. Although borderline spoiler, it's important to know 1 fact about the play, as it plays into the mind of so many psychologists today when they speak about an Oedipal Complex, as in all young boys (kids?) fall in love with their mothers at some point. Essentially, Oedipus kills the King and marries the King's wife. Little does he know.... that was his father and she is his mother. Whaaaaattttt? How does that happen? Seriously... well, the plot is intricate, the history is insane... and it's only the first of three in this trilogy. Find a translation and read it. It's a little convoluted, and the language may be a bit metaphorical in too many places, but the characters and the plot is amazing!<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <u>About Me</u>
  </i>
</b><br />For those new to me or my reviews... here's the scoop: I read A LOT. I write A LOT. And now I blog A LOT. First the book review goes on Goodreads, and then I send it on over to my WordPress blog at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://thisismytruthnow.com">https://thisismytruthnow.com</a>, where you'll also find TV &amp; Film reviews, the revealing and introspective 365 Daily Challenge and lots of blogging about places I've visited all over the world. And you can find all my social media profiles to get the details on the who/what/when/where and my pictures. Leave a comment and let me know what you think. Vote in the poll and ratings. Thanks for stopping by.</span>

<div id="review_2281370831" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2281370831" />
    <a title="emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32879029-emma"><img alt="emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520568130p2/32879029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2281370831?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="emma" href="/user/show/32879029-emma">emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/23316615-oedipus-the-king">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=3-and-a-half-stars">3-and-a-half-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32879029-emma?shelf=classics">classics</a>,

<div id="review_1218498917" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1218498917" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1218498917?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=classical">classical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=re-reads">re-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1218498917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3686996670884734842"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426708740i/14083784._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>THE EYE THAT DIES</b>
</blockquote><br/><br/>I have not read Sophocles’ text recently, but listened to <br/><a href='http://www.audible.com/pd/Classics/Oedipus-the-King-Audiobook/B002VAEKEO/ref=a_search_c4_1_1_srTtl?qid=1426715397&sr=1-1' rel='nofollow noopener'> this </a> exceptional audio edition. Powerfully acted out, with an eerie chorus and dramatic music, it has been a superb experience.<br/><br/>I have come back to this play now in a roundabout way. As part of a Seminar on Aesthetics, <b>The Eye that Thinks</b>, imparted in the Prado Museum, we were prompted by our Professor <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/43345.F_lix_de_Az_a' title='Félix de Azúa' rel='nofollow noopener'>Félix de Azúa</a> to read Oedipus in a Hegelian framework. We had been discussing the contributions of Hegel to Aestheti</span>
  <span id="freeText3686996670884734842" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426708740i/14083784._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><blockquote>
  <b>THE EYE THAT DIES</b>
</blockquote><br><br>I have not read Sophocles’ text recently, but listened to <br><a href="http://www.audible.com/pd/Classics/Oedipus-the-King-Audiobook/B002VAEKEO/ref=a_search_c4_1_1_srTtl?qid=1426715397&amp;sr=1-1" rel="nofollow noopener"> this </a> exceptional audio edition. Powerfully acted out, with an eerie chorus and dramatic music, it has been a superb experience.<br><br>I have come back to this play now in a roundabout way. As part of a Seminar on Aesthetics, <b>The Eye that Thinks</b>, imparted in the Prado Museum, we were prompted by our Professor <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/43345.F_lix_de_Az_a" title="Félix de Azúa" rel="nofollow noopener">Félix de Azúa</a> to read Oedipus in a Hegelian framework. We had been discussing the contributions of Hegel to Aesthetics, and he wanted us to visit the play and think of the role of Sphinx and the significance of Thebes.<br><br>In Hegel’s aesthetic system he identified Greek sculptures as the apex of what art could achieve in its quest of perfect and supreme beauty. Earlier architecture and art were still immature attempts. For example, the large Egyptian monuments were undertakings in which matter still prevailed over Geist. When Hegel saw some Kouroi in Munich (now in the Glyptotech), specimens of very early Greek art, he was struck by the significance of the step in this walking man.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426708740i/14083785.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In Egyptian representations of humans, legs are seen in profile. They depicted stability, while the Greek marble in Munich man was striding forward. The Kouros, although still using Egyptian conventions presented something very new. It embodied gesture. And Hegel thought that art should strive to represent movement. <br><br>The conceptual step of the Kouros, an awakening out of immobility, separated the worlds of the two Thebes: the one in Boetia in ancient Greece from the one up the Nile in ancient Egypt. In Greece Geist was finally on the move.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426708740i/14083786._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>If Hegel favoured Greek sculpture, he found that Greek drama could offer an additional dimension to sculpted beauty as the unfolding of time could be represented as well. For him Greek tragedy had invoked the greatest aesthetic power.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426708740i/14083787.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Hegel had also understood the Egyptian Sphinx as the first instance of the representation of human emerging out, liberating himself, from his animal nature. In this reading of Oedipus Rex as I have tried to keep on some sort of Hegelian glasses (and forget about the pervasive Freudian interpretation), I have seen the solution of Oedipus to the riddle of the Sphinx, and the consequent dissolution of the curse on Thebes and the destruction of the monster, as the emergence of humanity over its previous servitudes and imprisonment. <br><br>And yet, this conquered freedom also brought the possibility of unwilled intention or of the unintended will and the impossibility of unlearning what one already knows.<br><br>Trapped in this situation Oedipus attempt to escape his knowledge by doing away with his eyes, could only bring death.<br><br>As the chorus chants: it is the only liberation.<br><br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426708814i/14083791.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>---<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="3686996670884734842" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343669124" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4128997906069254129">Oedipus of Sophocles is a great work of art written by a great poet,this play symbolizes for the human misery and despair...<br/>the torments of the human soul,the innocence and guilt,Wisdom Out of Suffering and Fate that determines many things no matter how we struggle to change it....<br/>Oedipus hears about his dreadful fate from the Delphic oracle and flees from Corinth. But instead of fleeing from his fate he runs into it...<br/><br/>Oedipus a passionate heart,who ask questions and take risks,has all the quali</span>
  <span id="freeText4128997906069254129" style="display:none">Oedipus of Sophocles is a great work of art written by a great poet,this play symbolizes for the human misery and despair...<br />the torments of the human soul,the innocence and guilt,Wisdom Out of Suffering and Fate that determines many things no matter how we struggle to change it....<br />Oedipus hears about his dreadful fate from the Delphic oracle and flees from Corinth. But instead of fleeing from his fate he runs into it...<br /><br />Oedipus a passionate heart,who ask questions and take risks,has all the qualities of a great man...he has gone through sudden shifts on the course of his life and lets every situation control him....<br /><br />Despite his flaws, Oedipus is a good person who seeks the truth no matter how devastating. and who accept the responsibility for his actions.....<br /><br />At the end of the play, Oedipus accepts his fate as well as the punishment given to him ....<br /><br />He had promised to exile the one who is responsible for the plague , and he fulfills his promise even if he himself is the one to be exiled. By mercilessly punishing himself, he becomes a great hero...<br />who has a Respect for Justice ....<br /><br />Jocasta, on the other hand, appears as a person who would rather control the situation. She reveals that she is more mature than Oedipus and even reveals a maternal side towards him. This is evident in the way she tries to stop Oedipus from investigating further into the mystery of his birth. At this point, she has realized the possibility that Oedipus may be her son. She would rather let the dreadful fact remain a mystery then let it ruin their lives<br />The entwined sheets with which she hangs herself symbolize the double life she has led........<br /><br />Oedipus tragic position and his trial to elude the prophecies and to challenge his Fate, that was inevitable as he at last fails, but just having the courage to attempt , makes him a true hero. <br /><br />This play raises a question,when someone is trying to avoid doing things. Does he have free will or the ability to choose his own path or is everything in life predetermined? <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4128997906069254129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_400295970" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400295970" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400295970?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=plays">plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400295970" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17842178768480357603">“I want a girl, just like the girl, that married dear old Dad”—Oedipus<br/><br/>But, why accept a copy when you can get the real thing?!<br/><br/>I read this because 1) recently I had read (out of order) Antigone and thought I might reread the whole trilogy; 2) I plan to see a local production of the play and 3) I had some vague notion that the play, about a king, might throw some literary light on our current American King and kingly conduct. I listened to it, though I had to start over 3-4 times because the reade</span>
  <span id="freeText17842178768480357603" style="display:none">“I want a girl, just like the girl, that married dear old Dad”—Oedipus<br /><br />But, why accept a copy when you can get the real thing?!<br /><br />I read this because 1) recently I had read (out of order) Antigone and thought I might reread the whole trilogy; 2) I plan to see a local production of the play and 3) I had some vague notion that the play, about a king, might throw some literary light on our current American King and kingly conduct. I listened to it, though I had to start over 3-4 times because the reader was literally the worst reader of a text I have ever encountered; I would have preferred hearing Siri read the text to this play. I thought he was deliberately doing a flat, comic interpretation at times. He made the already formal language especially stilted and dry. I already preferred Antigone, but this experience made me like the play a little less. But hey, it’s still a classical tragedy that has been read for centuries and a good story.<br /><br />Oedipus is the first in the trilogy, also known as Oedipus Tyrannus. As you maybe know from the fact that even if you haven’t read the play, Freud’s “Oedipus complex” comes from this play: O became king of Thebes after fulfilling a prophecy made by blind Teresias that he would kill his father, Laius, and marry his mother, Jocasta (though he didn’t really know she was his mother, to his credit).<br /><br />[Let’s pause here and consider this incest question with respect to the presidency; Trump said even if he murdered someone that his base would still vote for him; if he had married his, let’s say, daughter, Ivanka (and you’ve heard what he said about dating her, so this is not so far-fetched), would his base have still voted for him? Consider.] <br /><br />[Another kingly reflection: Oedipus means “swollen feet,” as his feet had been tightly bound by Laius. Trump is known for having tiny appendages; write a 3-5 page paper reflecting on the implications of appendage size regarding kingly pride.]<br /><br />So one serious theme the play takes up is the relationship of the individual to the state (as happens in Antigone, too, with King Creon). Each king makes a decision that his subjects question or disobey; each king misconstrues disagreement as rebellion. While Creon saw his mistake, Oedipus refuses to listen to anyone who disagrees with him. Question: Is listening a useful property in a leader?<br /><br />The blind prophet Tiresias “sees” more than Oedipus, who cannot “see” the truth because his excessive pride has made him “blind.” I leave it to you whether this insight into political leadership from roughly two dozen centuries ago has trickled down to any political leaders we know, but “pride” is Oedipus’s tragic flaw (see Aristotle), which “goeth before his fall.” Discuss. <br /><br />This play really builds up a lot of steam by its (tragic) end. And it's probably the greatest classical tragedy, by reputation, so in spite of the fact that I connected a bit better to Antigone (because I like that character in the play) better, I still this is a play well-worth reading about politics and human nature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17842178768480357603" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_182425818" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182425818" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/997600-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1309718302p2/997600.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182425818?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/997600-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671888046" href="/book/show/1579127.Oedipus_The_King">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182425818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16168015933739843108">I'd say "spoiler alert" but it seems ridiculous . . . <br/><br/>I've taught this play for years, and I think this year I finally decided what makes this play great. My students never feel sympathy for Macbeth, but they do for Oedipus, and that always used to bother me. They whine in their teenage attitudinal voices, "But he didn't know that was his father." I always respond, "So it's ok to KILL PEOPLE if they're not your father?!" <br/><br/>In identifying with Oedipus, they forget the nature of the atrocities he co</span>
  <span id="freeText16168015933739843108" style="display:none">I'd say "spoiler alert" but it seems ridiculous . . . <br /><br />I've taught this play for years, and I think this year I finally decided what makes this play great. My students never feel sympathy for Macbeth, but they do for Oedipus, and that always used to bother me. They whine in their teenage attitudinal voices, "But he didn't know that was his father." I always respond, "So it's ok to KILL PEOPLE if they're not your father?!" <br /><br />In identifying with Oedipus, they forget the nature of the atrocities he committed, and that is where the greatness of this play lies - in creating a character who does horrible things, but who never seems like a monster to his audience: to them, he's just a human with human failings. He is essentially a good man, one who tries to help people, who makes tragic mistakes. In this sense my students mirror the feelings of the people of Thebes: the chorus defends Oedipus to the end, unable to believe evil of this great man who saved them once and is trying to save them again. When Oedipus is revealed as not being the son of Polybus and possibly the son of slaves, the chorus believes then that he must be the child of a god, for who else could spawn such a great man?<br /><br />But Oedipus' humanity lies in his course of action which spirals out of his control - and that, I think, is the element in Oedipus with which my students identify. Oedipus becomes a victim of the unforseen consequences of his own actions. These actions, of course, are fueled by his own pride - arrogance to think he can avoid Apollo's prophecy, and pride turned to anger in being pushed off the road when he feels the other driver should be giving way to his own great self (Ancient Greek road rage!). He may have been doomed since before birth by Apollo's curse on his family, but Oedipus creates his own problems. In believing he can avoid Apollo's prophecy, he shows us that he thinks he has outsmarted the gods, that he is greater than the gods. This, then, is the ultimate hubris and his ultimate undoing.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16168015933739843108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788807820069" href="/book/show/9759805-edipo-re">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34720769-amaranta?shelf=classici-greci">classici-greci</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34720769-amaranta?shelf=teatro">teatro</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/34720769-amaranta?shelf=miti">miti</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2419795573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5130296606999759226">“ <i> Chi tra gli umani, chi riceve più felicità di quanta ne basti per sembrare felice? </i>”.<br/>Mi sono ritrovata a voler rileggere questo dramma, affrontato più volte sui banchi di scuola, per i numerosi richiami che Pamuk ne fa nel suo “ la donna dai capelli rossi”. Volevo capire l’ossessione che il personaggio ha per questo mito e niente è meglio che ritornare sui propri passi. <br/>Edipo è l’archetipo del male, che si perpetua senza coscienza, l’uomo che uccide senza saperlo il padre, colui che lo ha gen</span>
  <span id="freeText5130296606999759226" style="display:none">“ <i> Chi tra gli umani, chi riceve più felicità di quanta ne basti per sembrare felice? </i>”.<br />Mi sono ritrovata a voler rileggere questo dramma, affrontato più volte sui banchi di scuola, per i numerosi richiami che Pamuk ne fa nel suo “ la donna dai capelli rossi”. Volevo capire l’ossessione che il personaggio ha per questo mito e niente è meglio che ritornare sui propri passi. <br />Edipo è l’archetipo del male, che si perpetua senza coscienza, l’uomo che uccide senza saperlo il padre, colui che lo ha generato, sangue del suo sangue e carne della sua carne, pur facendo di tutto perché ciò non succeda, e che giace con la madre da cui genera dei figli, suoi stessi fratelli dunque. <br />Quello che mi ha sempre colpito di Edipo è il fatto che non si uccida, ma che scelga di accecarsi. Morire sarebbe una liberazione per chi tanto ha commesso. Invece continuare a vivere, senza più vedere le bellezze del mondo, in un buio senza fine, gli ricorderà sempre la sua colpa ed è questo il modo per espiarla davanti agli occhi del mondo e degli stessi dei. <br />Il ritmo è veloce, incalzante come la verità, il coro ammalia prima con parole splendide, poi con accuse feroci: <br />“<i> Le sue mani sono rosse di sangue!</i>” e rimanda a messaggi sibillini: <br />“<i> Sospeso nell’aria/Non decifro il presente/Il passato mi è oscuro”</i><br />Penso alla Pizia di Dürrenmatt che lancia ad Edipo un vaticinio assurdo solo per toglierselo dai piedi come se quel “verbo” una volta emesso dalla sua bocca sia di per sé motivo di realizzazione del destino di Edipo. <br />Imponente la figura di Tiresia, interprete del volere di Apollo:<br />“<i> EDIPO: pensi di poter parlare così in eterno e rallegrartene ? <br />TIRESIA: Si, se esiste la forza della verità.<br />E tu che ora vedi chiaro vedrai solo tenebra</i>”<br />Colpiscono le immagini di una città in balia della peste come una nave squassata dalle onde, e di un Ade nero che si nutre di singhiozzi e lamenti.<br />Una lettura più consapevole, oggi. <br />“<i> Ubris genera tiranni.” </i> Mai frase fu più vera.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5130296606999759226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2257561818" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2257561818" />
    <a title="david" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67794375-david"><img alt="david" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597156400p2/67794375.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2257561818?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="david" class="user" itemprop="url" name="david" href="/user/show/67794375-david">david</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/26833369-king-oedipus">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/67794375-david?shelf=plays-drama">plays-drama</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2257561818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9036600380360643658">I really do not like my mother.<br/> <br/>I realize that moms (mums for the English) have many hats to wear. There is the tumult she has with the husband who never listens to her, and the children who end up at the principal’s office, and the clothes that need mending, and the purveying and construction of victuals to meet everyone’s different palate. Got it. I cannot even imagine what extra toll and toil the 1960’s will bring on these unappreciated females. But that is still years away. I like to focus o</span>
  <span id="freeText9036600380360643658" style="display:none">I really do not like my mother.<br /> <br />I realize that moms (mums for the English) have many hats to wear. There is the tumult she has with the husband who never listens to her, and the children who end up at the principal’s office, and the clothes that need mending, and the purveying and construction of victuals to meet everyone’s different palate. Got it. I cannot even imagine what extra toll and toil the 1960’s will bring on these unappreciated females. But that is still years away. I like to focus on the now. Why? Sounds good, I think. Please pass the black and white corn on the cob.<br /><br />But some mothers are…well…mothers. I include mine in this subsection. She is so bad I do not hesitate to walk little old ladies across the interstate. She is so bad that occasionally I will borrow a granny's walker for few minutes and ask them to lean against a wall while I spin it around. She is so bad that I consciously walk through the make-up departments at big chains and offer an ersatz opinion to elderly mothers on their rouge or their eyeliner or whatever it is that they buy for their faces.<br /> <br />I will say, in passing, “No, the lipstick is too vermillion.” Of course, they will think it over, because what kind of man knows the word vermillion. “Periwinkle eye-shadow? In the summer?” will cause them to blush vermillion, as I roll my eyes (drama). You can never use the word vermillion enough, I have always thought. It’s the opposite of pizza. One slice of it after forty-years old and you have gained twelve pounds. Two slices, straight away to a triple bypass at the local emergency room. Three slices and a Parson delivers the box, and waits.<br /> <br />And even after many attempts at self-normalizing behavior, I still do not like my mother. I know, it is against one of those ten commandments, but we just do not get along. It happens. Why are you trying to make me feel guilty?<br /><br />“Tell me about your mother, david?”<br />“Oh no, not again.”<br />“Is she pretty?”<br />“What?”<br />“Is she malicious?”<br />“Well…”<br />“Oops. Sorry. The session is up. See you again next Monday at nine?”<br />“Cannot. I must give a speech at the Greater NY Jockey association. The topic is ‘Organic Horse Feed, Worth the Cost?’”<br />“You sound resentful?”<br />“I thought our time was over? Hmm.”<br />“One hundred dollars, please.”<br />“A little resentful, now.”<br /><br />Sophocles, what’s your deal? This is Greece. It is friggin’ hot here. There are no “Saturday Morning food fairs” and you do not like fishing? Why not go to Santorini for the week? They are having a sale on last year’s togas and this year’s newest flip-flops.<br /> <br />No. Instead, you go off on your own, chisel in hand and rocks on the ground, no one around for hectares, and you start writing a story about a boy and his mommy, in an intimate way? Perhaps you also wrote for Penthouse forum? Our Athenian audience is all men, this is not Off-Off Broadway. They ain't going to like it. Well, maybe the politicians...<br /><br />Listen to me, your friend Socratberg. Go to the nearest dispensary and buy yourself some hybrid hemlock. Have it with a little vino. Don’t forget it may take an hour before the effects set in.<br /><br />Okay, basta. Y’all know how the story goes. A tragedy indeed for the son/husband and serendipity for Sophocles, who, so far has about twenty-five hundred years run on this production.<br /><br />Next time, take my mother, please.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9036600380360643658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer12763672943840645034">أوديب ملكاً - سوفوكليس<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/>لم أتوقع أنني سأتأثر بهذه التراجيديا بعدما سمعتها مرات ومرات وكدت أن أفقد دهشة الحكاية لولا قرائتي لهذه المسرحية , كونها من أكثر القصص والأساطير اليونانية شهرة على الإطلاق, قلت آن لي أن أقرأ هذه المسرحية الشهيرة التي أستوحاها سوفوكليس من الاسطورة اليونانية المذكورة في ملحمة اليونان القديمة المعروفة بالإلياذة ..<br/><br/><br/>جسدها سوفوكليس بعبقرية لا توصف وتراجيدية وفلسفة عميقة يشهد لها التاريخ ومازالت تثير الدهشة في قلب القارئ , وهي قصة أوديب الذي قتل أباه وتزوج من أمه بعد ذلك من دون أن </span>
  <span id="freeText12763672943840645034" style="display:none">أوديب ملكاً - سوفوكليس<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />لم أتوقع أنني سأتأثر بهذه التراجيديا بعدما سمعتها مرات ومرات وكدت أن أفقد دهشة الحكاية لولا قرائتي لهذه المسرحية , كونها من أكثر القصص والأساطير اليونانية شهرة على الإطلاق, قلت آن لي أن أقرأ هذه المسرحية الشهيرة التي أستوحاها سوفوكليس من الاسطورة اليونانية المذكورة في ملحمة اليونان القديمة المعروفة بالإلياذة ..<br /><br /><br />جسدها سوفوكليس بعبقرية لا توصف وتراجيدية وفلسفة عميقة يشهد لها التاريخ ومازالت تثير الدهشة في قلب القارئ , وهي قصة أوديب الذي قتل أباه وتزوج من أمه بعد ذلك من دون أن يعلم ذلك , حدث ذلك بعد الوحي الذي أوحاه أبولون لوالد أوديب الذي حاول جاهداً التخلص من أبنه وتفادي المصير المشؤوم الذي قال به الوحي, ولكن ماذا عسى أن ينقذ الأب إذا كانت الأقدار قد سلطت ذلك المصير القاسي على أوديب وكيف يتفادى المرء حدثاً قد كان مكتوباً ..<br /><br /><br />تدور الأحداث وتنقلب الأيام فيقع الأثم وتتحطم مسارات البشر أمام جحيم القدر ويعلم أوديب أنه لم يولد إلا لكي يقع فريسة لتلك النبوءة المشؤومة, يصرخ أوديب , يتألم كأن جنون العالم قد صب على رأسه , يعلم حينها أنه أشقى البشر على وجه الأرض..<br /><br /><br /><br /><b><br />" أنظروا, يا أهالي ثيبا , ها هو ذا أوديب الحاذق في حل الألغاز الشهيرة<br /> والذي صار أول بني الإنسان , لم يكن أحد في المدينة يتأمل مصيره إلا ويحسده .. <br />أما اليوم , فها هو قد وقع في هاوية من الشقاء الرهيب..<br />فحذار إذن من أن نصف إنساناً بأنه سعيد , قبل أن يكون قد إجتاز نهاية عمره دون أن يكون قد عانى مصيبة !! "<br /></b><br /><br /><br /><br />هذه واحدة من أكثر القصص تراجيدية وعبثية التي ستقرأها في حياتك , كيف لا وهي تتعلق بمصير إنسان قد حكمت عليه الأقدار بمصير كالجحيم وحياة شقية أكثر سوداوية من كل تصوراتنا العبثية حتى ..</span>

    <a title="Oziel Bispo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56294126-oziel-bispo"><img alt="Oziel Bispo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492105953p2/56294126.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2602106529?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oziel Bispo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oziel Bispo" href="/user/show/56294126-oziel-bispo">Oziel Bispo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9724405176" href="/book/show/11178524-rei-dipo">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2602106529" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14416323115794305412">Baseando-se no famoso mito de Édipo muito conhecido na época, o poeta Sófocles  compõe Édipo Rei , uma peça teatral que melhor representou esse mito e que ficou em primeiro lugar entre as três finalistas. Conta a luta de Édipo para descobrir a verdade sobre a morte do antigo rei  Laio da qual é acusado, e também sua luta para descobrir a verdade sobre si mesmo, verdade esta que é horrenda, surpreendente. Conta também sua luta para fugir das sinas profetizadas pelos Oráculos,quando ele  era ainda</span>
  <span id="freeText14416323115794305412" style="display:none">Baseando-se no famoso mito de Édipo muito conhecido na época, o poeta Sófocles  compõe Édipo Rei , uma peça teatral que melhor representou esse mito e que ficou em primeiro lugar entre as três finalistas. Conta a luta de Édipo para descobrir a verdade sobre a morte do antigo rei  Laio da qual é acusado, e também sua luta para descobrir a verdade sobre si mesmo, verdade esta que é horrenda, surpreendente. Conta também sua luta para fugir das sinas profetizadas pelos Oráculos,quando ele  era ainda uma criança , que diziam que Édipo iria matar o seu próprio pai e casar com sua mãe.<br /><br />Realmente uma tragédia, pois Édipo está num caminho sem saída e sua família não facilitou nem um pouco sua vida..<br />Às vezes é melhor não saber, é melhor estar cego para a verdade, você vai saber o porquê de eu estar dizendo isso ao ler esse livro maravilhoso.<br />Abaixo cito um texto de Francisco Daudt que comenta com prioridade a respeito disso:<br /><br />“Agora eu te pergunto: Édipo merece essa fama maldita? A meu ver, ele foi colocado numa arapuca inescapável, metido num problema que não era dele, era dos pais. Não deixaram Édipo viver sua própria vida. Ele foi atrelado à vida dos pais, aos assuntos dos pais. E mesmo os reis de Corinto, seus pais de adoção, deixaram uma armadilha esperando por ele, ao sonegar-lhe<br />sua verdadeira história.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="14416323115794305412" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1043384456" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16720946482286354426">نمايشنامه ى مشهور اديپ شهريار، يكى از بزرگترين تراژدى هاى يونان باستان.<br/>از لحاظ داستان، واقعاً اعجاب انگيزه. داستان ابتدا با معمايى شروع ميشه كه نجات شهر "تِبس" از نابودى به حل اون بسته است، ولى هيچ كس جوابش رو نميدونه. بعد كم كم كه معما حل ميشه، پاسخ وحشتناك و تراژيكش آشكار ميشه و نجات شهر از نابودى، به بهاى بسيار سنگينى حاصل ميشه. اين روش طرح معما و حل مرحله به مرحله ش، بسيار شبيه به داستان هاى جنايى امروزيه، و از خيلى از اين داستان ها به مراتب بهتره.<br/>اما اثر، طبعاً به مقتضاى زمان نگارشش، مشكلات</span>
  <span id="freeText16720946482286354426" style="display:none">نمايشنامه ى مشهور اديپ شهريار، يكى از بزرگترين تراژدى هاى يونان باستان.<br />از لحاظ داستان، واقعاً اعجاب انگيزه. داستان ابتدا با معمايى شروع ميشه كه نجات شهر "تِبس" از نابودى به حل اون بسته است، ولى هيچ كس جوابش رو نميدونه. بعد كم كم كه معما حل ميشه، پاسخ وحشتناك و تراژيكش آشكار ميشه و نجات شهر از نابودى، به بهاى بسيار سنگينى حاصل ميشه. اين روش طرح معما و حل مرحله به مرحله ش، بسيار شبيه به داستان هاى جنايى امروزيه، و از خيلى از اين داستان ها به مراتب بهتره.<br />اما اثر، طبعاً به مقتضاى زمان نگارشش، مشكلات خودش رو هم داره. از جمله اين كه تمام نمايشنامه فقط با ديالوگ روايت ميشه و چيزى "نمايش" داده نميشه. <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[حتا خودكشى و کور شدن ادیپوس هم، که اوج تراژيك داستانه، فقط از قول يه خادم نقل ميشه و ما چيزى نمى بينيم. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
